it wouldn't be possible anyway, and for a reason no one ever talks about. Houses are constructions, they are made up of parts held together with nails and screws and glue and joinery. That means you would be putting yourself at the mercy of sheering forces. And the sheering force of wood (which makes up the majority of Carl's house) is WAY lower than the force needed to lift said house. If the balloons have sufficient lift to hoist the house, they have sufficient lift to tear the house apart. This is particularly true because in the movie, Carl has all the balloon strings tied to the grate of his fireplace. IRL the balloons would simply pull the grate and the bolts securing it, right out of the floor and rocket off to space with it.

That amount of force generated from the Balloon Estimate would be EQUAL to the Houses weight. So there's be just as much force pulling the house UP, as there would be pulling the house DOWN. So the House would have a Neutral Buoyancy in the air, so you'd need A LOT more balloons in order to actually achieve lift off. Not to mention, the amount of balloons required to actually rip the house from its foundation. So probably TWICE as many balloons would be required to generate a good take off speed, plus enough force to rip it from the foundation.

Something else not added to the movie is that helium balloons can leak or lose lift over time. You would get to a point where no matter how many balloons you fill, the lifting force wouldn‚Äôt get any higher, as the ones already filled with helium are decreasing in lift while you‚Äôre only replacing the lost lift force instead of adding to it. Myth busters did an experiment where they filled a lot of balloons with helium to see if you could lift a child. But as they started to get close to the number of balloons needed, the ones already filled had lost helium and therefore lift so more balloons were needed to be filled to replace them.

Up is not the kind of film that is meant to take any steps towards realism. Stylistically, that wouldn't make any sense. It's an escapist movie that manages to connect with people in the real world by showing universal emotions and struggles in a creative way. Imagine if Speed Racer attempted to show realistic car races, or Star Wars made light speed realistic. It wouldn't work. What matters is if the rules of the universe being portrayed are consistent, not if they are identical to our own. Talking dogs aren't realistic either, or using a handful of balloons and a leafblower to fly into a blimp. But the weirdness of this Pixar world helps to tell a powerful story.

Carl would‚Äôve had to lift the entire house off the foundation to disconnect it. Then he‚Äôd have to have metal supports and attaching points for the balloons installed all over the house to support the weight of the house, at the same time adding tons upon tons of extra weight. Ultimately anyway you want to do it the house would crumble under its own weight. You‚Äôd probably have to have clusters of balloons separated and connected to the house via steel cable; also adding tons of weight. It would actually be impossible to float a house that weight with balloons, because as you get more balloons you begin to need more fishing line to connect them to the house, and the length of line would end up weighing more than 9 grams (the calculated lifting force of a helium balloon) well before you actually had enough balloons, from there, adding more balloons would have a negative effect, and so the house could never fly.

I remember when Mythbusters tested something like this. Their conclusion was that, though you can theoretically lift anything with enough balloons, you're limited by the amount of balloons you you can collect. At a certain point the pressure of the balloons pressing against each other will cause them to pop.
Hailing from Utah, I actually had a chance to visit the Up House replica. Where the movie didn't have a foundation, the IRL one did, and even had a basement added because the Salt Lake Valley lies on a major fault line. It's possible that your calculations included the lower levels. While the feat in Up is still obviously impossible financially, your calculations might be off.

There is also one more problem. The balloons are subject to diffusion. The reason why balloons don‚Äôt last that long when you release them in your house is that they diffuse from the pressures; outside air comes in, helium comes out. This would make the balloons more heavy and unable to do their job. Thank you for coming to my Ted talk.
Yea true, but in the movie, the house literally RIPS OUT THE GROUND. These balloons would gently lift him off the ground, so you would have to consider the force pushing the house down while calculating how much force you would need to lift it up. Also, you can't keep going up forever. In hot air balloons and other aerial balloon-like vehicles, there's always a method to reduce the amount of force pulling the object up so that it can float in midair, instead of flying all the way up into the sky where the lack of air pressure would burst the balloons. Yeah, I can buy the science behind the theory of lifting your house into the sky, with helium filled balloons, except for a couple of issues.  First, where on your house would you tie all those balloons to get it to lift the entire structure without ripping it apart. A house, is not built from a single piece of material, at least no houses I've ever heard of, they're built using thousands of individual pieces of material connected together. If the balloons have enough force to lift the house, wouldn't they also have enough force to pull it apart? Secondly, what about the balloons themselves, if you've ever observed a helium balloon after a few days, they don't rise as high as they did when they were new. I think, its because the helium leaks out of it, probably through the knot, or even from the expanded material of the latex - I mean, it does go from opaque to translucent as it's inflated - and as the helium atoms leak, the oxygen forces it back down to earth. And, let's look at the material itself, latex is really easy to acquire a static charge, just look at the balloon trick. A few passes over a fuzzy surface and it clings to your hair, imagine the charge that air molecules would induce when they pass around the balloons, talk about becoming a target for lightning.

Additional problem that wasn‚Äôt brought up: space. This is done so scientifically believably but I believe you used a VERY wrong estimate for amount of string needed. The 300.000 miles of string would mean about 15 meters per balloon. However, 9 liters times 31.000.000+ balloons adds up to over 280.000 cubic meters of balloons. That's over 65x65x65 meters, meaning that an average length of sting from the bottom would be about 35-40 meters, if not more.  That both adds more weight to the balloons, so that you might need some more of them, and mostly, that adds about 3-4 million dollars in extra costs.  Truly, the precise numbers in the video are entertaining, but when you're so much off on a fundamental calculation, that's pretty stupid.
Also you'd waste most of the worlds helium supply making all the scientists who use helium for experiment (like astrophysicists who are building cooled satellites)  very mad at you
Plot twist making a train track and run a train to Paradise falls is cheaper than balloons
Love your theories MatPat, but I'm still not convinced it's possible to lift the house at all. First, the length of the fishing line is drastically underestimated at 50 ft. When you have over 30 million balloons, the average length of line is going to be significantly higher than 50 ft, so you would need more balloons to account for that. Depending on how those numbers work out, it could get to the point where the weight of the fishing line is so significant that the balloons are no longer providing any lift. Based on your numbers, I think the balloon would start to be weighed down by fishing lines at a length of about 750 ft. Given the amount of balloons needed and the shear volume of space needed for all of them, I think that is a very real possibility. Second, where would you tie all the strings? If we're going by the method in the movie, attaching them to the fireplace grate, they would definitely not all fit there. Not to mention, there wouldn't be enough room in the chimney for all of those lines, so they would have to be attached to the outside of the house or come in through the doors and windows. Feasible, probably, but it would be a mess. And, of course, the anchor points would have to be somewhat evenly distributed or the house wouldn't fly straight or the house would just completely fall apart mid-flight. Third, the amount of force needed to rip the house from the foundation is not accounted for and it is pretty significant. Fourth, given the amount of time it would take to fill all of the balloons, many of them would deflate in the process, so you would need a lot of people working on this to mitigate that. With the number of people that would have to work on this (I would say minimum 10,000) and the amount of equipment needed, I can only see that leading to a disaster, especially given the dangers of working with helium, not only because of the possibility of the cans exploding, but also because of the dangers of ingesting helium. More incidents means more time means more deflated balloons. And of course, I don't think lifting the statue of liberty would be possible either for similar reasons.

I was skeptical of the strength of the balloons and thought they might pop so I did some calculations.  Using F=M√óA  260 000 kg ( matt pads numbers) √ó 9.8 (gravitational acceleration)  This give us 2 548 000 Newtons of force downwards. A Ballon can take 10n of force before it pops(Google told me that so im working on their numbers) To put that in perspective an object thats a little over 1kg puts out 10n gravitational force. To be safe lets work on 9n. Because the balloons float they will distribute the weight of the house equally. So 31 150 319 balloons (again Matt's numbers) could withstand 9√ó31 150 319 newtons of force... this translates to 28¬†607¬†435kg weight pilling down on all the ballons to pop them all. Boy way I wrong about my skepticism.

Hey Matpat! You know all those space movies? Y'know, all the ones with space travel? Like of any kind?  ln(0.0099158/760) = (349.6/0.008314)(1/5184 - 1/X)  ln -11.246942 = (349.6/0.008314)(1/5184 - 1/X)  Solve first parenthesis on the right. -11.246942 = (42049.5549)(1/5184 - 1/X)  Divide left by first parenthesis on right. -2.67540331e^-4 = (1/5184) - (1/X)  Subtract 1/5184 from left -4.604415657 = 1/X  Cross multiply 1 = -4.60441567X  Divide both sides by -4.60441567 X = -2171.82825  X is the boiling point of iron in degrees Fahrenheit at the air pressure of outer space.  The cosmic background temperature is -455 degrees Fahrenheit, and solar winds range from thousands to millions of degrees Fahrenheit.  I used this website for reference to the equation I used: https://socratic.org/questions/how-do-you-calculate-boiling-point-at-different-pressures  P.S. In case you didn't notice, at the air pressure available in outer space, even solid iron is vaporizing at temperatures far below what a space craft would experience in the shadow of our Earth, let alone entertaining the idea of capturing solar energy with solar panels.

You'd need a heck of a lot more balloons just to make up for the loss of helium in the ones blown up earlier. And you would need to keep replacing them as you flew unless you somehow travel at high speed and dont pop any... meaning you would need even more balloons to accommodate the weight of the stuff you need to replace balloons as you fly.   Its like jetpacks and rockets. You want to go farther, so u need more fuel. But in order to carry more fuel, you need to burn more fuel. Making any long distance travel simply unreasonable and non financially sustainable.

Another thing about helium balloons is the way they react to air temperatures. Cold air, like what one might run into at high altitudes, causes the balloons to shrink and lose their ability to stay airborne. Meanwhile warm/hot air will make them expand and eventually pop. Either way, as far as I know there is no possible way that Carl's house would stay in the air long enough to reach Paradise Falls. fishing line has a strength rating (30lb test, 100-lb test, etc) and comes in 2 basic styles--monofilament (it's a single material extruded into fine strands, kinda of like a spaghetti noodle) and braided (multiple lines twisted and woven together to basically make a really tiny rope).  There's a regulating authority that mediates fishing records and sets rules for events regarding what gear can be used (ie, fishing tournaments) that caps line strength at around 130-lb test for either style, but you can find monofilament line rated up to 300lb test and braided all the way up to 600+ lbs.  That said, if you're connecting all that line to rooftop connection points I'm betting the roof itself cannot support the weight of the house-material below it and it just rips off.  Instead, you would want to install a rigid support grid below the part of the house you want to lift and attach the balloons to that along the sides of the house.  This strategy is already efficiently employed when you wish to move a house from one location to another on a truck bed, but in these cases they just cut any connection to the foundation after installing extremely powerful hydraulic jack systems to hold the weight of the house up so it does not settle (on top of the movers cutting it away from the foundation).

at that many balloons I feel like the string issue is going to be much more severe than even what you estimated. eventually, with that many balloons, you'll need so much string to get a balloon far enough out to fit that the string it's self would weigh more than the balloon would weigh, putting an effective cap on how big you can go with the volume of normal sized balloons.  Then there is the issue of friction, and pressure. The balloons in the middle would be getting squeezed by all the other balloons and as you add more balloons, that pressure will grow, eventually the balloons int he middle will be unable to endure the pressure and pop.
One thing I think you over looked is you did how many balloons it would take to keep the house in the air. But you would also need to account for ripping the house off the foundation. Houses are anchored into the ground. So you would need more balloons to rip it off the foundation

When it comes to how much a balloon holds, you didn't consider altitude.  You have to under-fill the balloon so it does not pop when it rises into thinner air. Also, the latex causes the inside to be pressurized, reducing the lift.  If you look at actual weather balloons, they are empty bags not stretchy rubber, and mostly slack when launched with just a little bubble of gas at the top. You should have, in Mythbusters' style, continued with doing what it takes to make it work.  What kind of large plastic bags might be available? As you mentioned 'how hard can it be' only used a shell of a house, brings up yet another potential problem; the house itself regarding it's structural integrity.   They didn't account for all the wiring & plumbing a normal house would have, let alone furniture, or even having a stockpile of food & water on hand to live through the trip; and holding the house up by it's roof instead of it's base would make sense for keeping it balanced upright, but you don't need an architecture degree to know that roofs aren't built to hold that kind weight.  Sure older eras have been built to last in comparison to today's due to the recent decades of encroaching industry philosophy of planned obsolescence, but lifting a house from its top floor & roof feels dubiously safe from falling apart no matter when it was made. Hey Matpat, nice Video, but you've made mistake in your assumptions. If you take 50ft of String, which is 15.24m of String, you get a Volume of space, where balloons can be of: 4/3 * pi * (15.24m)^3 = 14826.666m^3 A typical balloon has a Volume of 2.5 liters = 0.0025m^3 If we assume that the balloons would arrange themselves to leave no gaps inbetween each other, we can calculate the maximum Number of balloons we could use (not even regarding the volume of the House in our Sphere) with the chosen length of 50ft: 14826.666m^3/0.0025m^3 = 5,930,666 Therefore, we would need a longer line, to use the 30+million balloons. That would lead to us needing more balloons to carry the line. In actuality we would even need more, since the balloons shouldnt be under the House, nor within the House. Also the balloons wouldnt take up the whole space, there would be air holes everywhere.

If you take the 9 liters per balloon, multiply it by 30.000.000, then divide by 1000 and take the cube root, you find out that the balloons would fill a volume of 65*65*65 meters (210*210*210 feet) or a sphere with a diameter of 40 meters (130 feet).  This is assuming all balloons pack together perfectly, which is not the case. But even if it was, you'd need at least twice the length of string, which would double its weight and decrease a balloon's carrying capacity, which means you'd need even more balloons.

Even *if* you spent the ten years straight inflating and tying off balloons - the average balloon only stays inflated for no more than 3 DAYS (a week if the balloon is foil).To get them all filled in time, you'd need 12167 people to get the job done.  Presuming the 10 years is 1 person,  then 100 people would do the job in 1 year, then we multiply that by (365/3) to account for how many sets of 3 days are in  a year. And because that's got a decimal, round up to the next whole number.
